<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_sulfur" title="Template:Infobox sulfur"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_sulfur" title="Template talk:Infobox sulfur"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Infobox_sulfur&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div>&#x20;|&#x20;<a href="/wiki/List_of_data_references_for_chemical_elements" title="List of data references for chemical elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Sulfur</b> or <b>sulphur</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with symbol&#160;<b>S</b> and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>&#160;16. It is <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">abundant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">multivalent</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nonmetal" title="Nonmetal">nonmetallic</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">normal conditions</a>, sulfur atoms form cyclic octatomic molecules with a chemical formula <a href="/wiki/Octasulfur" title="Octasulfur">S<sub>8</sub></a>. Elemental sulfur is a bright yellow <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> solid at room temperature. Chemically, sulfur reacts with all elements except for <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iridium" title="Iridium">iridium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>.
</p><p>Sulfur is the tenth most common element by mass in the universe, and the fifth most common on Earth. Though sometimes found in pure, <a href="/wiki/Native_element_minerals" title="Native element minerals">native</a> form, sulfur on Earth usually occurs as <a href="/wiki/Sulfide_minerals" title="Sulfide minerals">sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_minerals" title="Sulfate minerals">sulfate minerals</a>. Being abundant in native form, sulfur was known in ancient times, being mentioned for its uses in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Ancient_China" title="History of China">China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, sulfur is called <b>brimstone</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwd_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwd-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Today, almost all elemental sulfur is produced as a byproduct of removing sulfur-containing contaminants from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>. The greatest commercial use of the element is the production of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> for sulfate and phosphate <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, and other chemical processes. The element sulfur is used in <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">matches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>. Many sulfur compounds are odoriferous, and the smells of odorized natural gas, skunk scent, grapefruit, and garlic are due to <a href="/wiki/Organosulfur" class="mw-redirect" title="Organosulfur">organosulfur</a> compounds. <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">Hydrogen sulfide</a> gives the characteristic odor to rotting eggs and other biological processes.
</p><p>Sulfur is an <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">essential element</a> for all life, but almost always in the form of <a href="/wiki/Organosulfur_compounds" title="Organosulfur compounds">organosulfur</a> compounds or metal sulfides. Three <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cystine" title="Cystine">cystine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>) and two vitamins (<a href="/wiki/Biotin" title="Biotin">biotin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">thiamine</a>) are organosulfur compounds. Many <a href="/wiki/Cofactor_(biochemistry)" title="Cofactor (biochemistry)">cofactors</a> also contain sulfur including <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thioredoxin" title="Thioredoxin">thioredoxin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93sulfur_protein" title="Iron–sulfur protein">iron–sulfur proteins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Disulfide" title="Disulfide">Disulfides</a>, S–S bonds, confer mechanical strength and insolubility of the protein <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratin</a>, found in outer skin, hair, and feathers. Sulfur is one of the core chemical elements needed for <a href="/wiki/Biochemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biochemical">biochemical</a> functioning and is an elemental <a href="/wiki/Macronutrient" class="mw-redirect" title="Macronutrient">macronutrient</a> for all living organisms.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning-sulfur.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Burning-sulfur.png/220px-Burning-sulfur.png" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Burning-sulfur.png/330px-Burning-sulfur.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Burning-sulfur.png 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning-sulfur.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When burned, sulfur melts to a blood-red liquid and emits a blue flame that is best observed in the dark.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur forms polyatomic molecules with different chemical formulas, the best-known allotrope being <a href="/wiki/Octasulfur" title="Octasulfur">octasulfur</a>, cyclo-S<sub>8</sub>. The <a href="/wiki/Point_group" title="Point group">point group</a> of cyclo-S<sub>8</sub> is D<sub>4d</sub> and its dipole moment is 0 D.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Octasulfur is a soft, bright-yellow solid that is odorless, but impure samples have an odor similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Match" title="Match">matches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> It melts at 115.21&#160;°C (239.38&#160;°F), boils at 444.6&#160;°C (832.3&#160;°F) and sublimes easily.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwd_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwd-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> At 95.2&#160;°C (203.4&#160;°F), below its melting temperature, cyclo-octasulfur changes from α-octasulfur to the β-<a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(materials_science)" title="Polymorphism (materials science)">polymorph</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The structure of the S<sub>8</sub> ring is virtually unchanged by this phase change, which affects the intermolecular interactions. Between its melting and boiling temperatures, octasulfur changes its allotrope again, turning from β-octasulfur to γ-sulfur, again accompanied by a lower density but increased <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> due to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Polymer" title="Polymer">polymers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> At higher temperatures, the viscosity decreases as depolymerization occurs. Molten sulfur assumes a dark red color above 200&#160;°C (392&#160;°F). The density of sulfur is about 2&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, depending on the allotrope; all of the stable allotropes are excellent electrical insulators.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur burns with a blue flame with formation of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>, which has a suffocating and irritating odor. Sulfur is insoluble in water but soluble in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> and, to a lesser extent, in other nonpolar organic solvents, such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>. The first and second ionization energies of sulfur are 999.6 and 2252&#160;kJ/mol, respectively. Despite such figures, the +2 oxidation state is rare, with +4 and +6 being more common. The fourth and sixth ionization energies are 4556 and 8495.8&#160;kJ/mol, the magnitude of the figures caused by electron transfer between orbitals; these states are only stable with strong oxidants such as <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
Sulfur reacts with nearly all other elements with the exception of gold, platinum, iridium, nitrogen, tellurium, iodine and the noble gases. Some of those reactions need elevated temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-WibergWiberg2001_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WibergWiberg2001-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allotropes">Allotropes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Allotropes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png/220px-Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png/330px-Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png/440px-Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="804" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyclooctasulfur-above-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The structure of the cyclooctasulfur molecule, S<sub>8</sub></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allotropes_of_sulfur" title="Allotropes of sulfur">Allotropes of sulfur</a></div>
<p>Sulfur forms over 30 solid <a href="/wiki/Allotropy" title="Allotropy">allotropes</a>, more than any other element.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Besides S<sub>8</sub>, several other rings are known.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Removing one atom from the crown gives S<sub>7</sub>, which is more deeply yellow than S<sub>8</sub>. <a href="/wiki/High-performance_liquid_chromatography" title="High-performance liquid chromatography">HPLC</a> analysis of "elemental sulfur" reveals an equilibrium mixture of mainly S<sub>8</sub>, but with S<sub>7</sub> and small amounts of S<sub>6</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Larger rings have been prepared, including S<sub>12</sub> and S<sub>18</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Amorphous" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorphous">Amorphous</a> or "plastic" sulfur is produced by rapid cooling of molten sulfur—for example, by pouring it into cold water. <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> studies show that the amorphous form may have a <a href="/wiki/Helix" title="Helix">helical</a> structure with eight atoms per turn. The long coiled polymeric molecules make the brownish substance <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">elastic</a>, and in bulk this form has the feel of crude rubber. This form is <a href="/wiki/Metastability_in_molecules" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastability in molecules">metastable</a> at room temperature and gradually reverts to crystalline molecular allotrope, which is no longer elastic. This process happens within a matter of hours to days, but can be rapidly catalyzed.
</p>
<div style="clear:left;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_sulfur" title="Isotopes of sulfur">Isotopes of sulfur</a></div>
<p>Sulfur has 25 known <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>, four of which are stable: <sup>32</sup>S (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6999949900000000000♠</span>94.99%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.26%</span>), <sup>33</sup>S (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6997750000000000000♠</span>0.75%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.02%</span>), <sup>34</sup>S (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6998425000000000000♠</span>4.25%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.24%</span>), and <sup>36</sup>S (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6996100000000000000♠</span>0.01%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.01%</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-b92_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-b92-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Other than <sup>35</sup>S, with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 87 days and formed in <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray_spallation" title="Cosmic ray spallation">cosmic ray spallation</a> of <sup>40</sup><a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">Ar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a> isotopes of sulfur have half-lives less than 3&#160;hours.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Sulfide_mineral" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulfide mineral">sulfide minerals</a> are precipitated, isotopic equilibration among solids and liquid may cause small differences in the δS-34 values of co-genetic minerals. The differences between minerals can be used to estimate the temperature of equilibration. The δ<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">C</a>-13 and δS-34 of coexisting <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_minerals" title="Carbonate minerals">carbonate minerals</a> and sulfides can be used to determine the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> and oxygen <a href="/wiki/Fugacity" title="Fugacity">fugacity</a> of the ore-bearing fluid during ore formation.
</p><p>In most <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a> ecosystems, sulfate is derived mostly from the atmosphere; weathering of ore minerals and evaporites contribute some sulfur. Sulfur with a distinctive isotopic composition has been used to identify pollution sources, and enriched sulfur has been added as a tracer in <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrologic</a> studies. Differences in the <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundances</a> can be used in systems where there is sufficient variation in the <sup>34</sup>S of ecosystem components. <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountain" class="mw-redirect" title="Rocky Mountain">Rocky Mountain</a> lakes thought to be dominated by atmospheric sources of sulfate have been found to have different <a href="/wiki/%CE%9434S" title="Δ34S">δ<sup>34</sup>S</a> values from lakes believed to be dominated by watershed sources of sulfate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_occurrence">Natural occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Natural occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded,_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.,_Hoskins_Mound,_Texas,_1a35438v.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg/220px-Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg/330px-Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg/440px-Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded%2C_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.%2C_Hoskins_Mound%2C_Texas%2C_1a35438v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6075" data-file-height="4550" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nearly_exhausted_sulphur_vat_from_which_railroad_cars_are_loaded,_Freeport_Sulphur_Co.,_Hoskins_Mound,_Texas,_1a35438v.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfur vat from which railroad cars are loaded, Freeport Sulphur Co., Hoskins Mound, Texas (1943)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg/220px-Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg/330px-Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg/440px-Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3196" data-file-height="3196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Io_highest_resolution_true_color.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most of the yellow and orange hues of <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> are due to elemental sulfur and sulfur compounds deposited by active <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg/220px-Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg/330px-Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg/440px-Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5616" data-file-height="3744" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kawah_Ijen_-East_Java_-Indonesia_-sulphur-31July2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man carrying sulfur blocks from <a href="/wiki/Kawah_Ijen" class="mw-redirect" title="Kawah Ijen">Kawah Ijen</a>, a volcano in East Java, Indonesia, 2009</div></div></div>
<p><sup>32</sup>S is created inside massive stars, at a depth where the temperature exceeds 2.5×10<sup>9</sup>&#160;K, by the <a href="/wiki/Silicon_burning" class="mw-redirect" title="Silicon burning">fusion</a> of one nucleus of silicon plus one nucleus of helium.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> As this is part of the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_process" title="Alpha process">alpha process</a> that produces elements in abundance, sulfur is the 10th most common element in the universe.
</p><p>Sulfur, usually as sulfide, is present in many types of <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a>. Ordinary chondrites contain on average 2.1% sulfur, and carbonaceous chondrites may contain as much as 6.6%. It is normally present as <a href="/wiki/Troilite" title="Troilite">troilite</a> (FeS), but there are exceptions, with carbonaceous chondrites containing free sulfur, sulfates and other sulfur compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The distinctive colors of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> moon <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a> are attributed to various forms of molten, solid and gaseous sulfur.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg/220px-Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg/330px-Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg/440px-Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fumarola_Vulcano.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfur occurs in <a href="/wiki/Fumaroles" class="mw-redirect" title="Fumaroles">fumaroles</a> such as this one in <a href="/wiki/Vulcano" title="Vulcano">Vulcano</a>, Italy</div></div></div>
<p>It is the fifth most common element by mass in the Earth. Elemental sulfur can be found near <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">hot springs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Volcanic">volcanic</a> regions in many parts of the world, especially along the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ring_of_Fire" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific Ring of Fire">Pacific Ring of Fire</a>; such volcanic deposits are currently mined in Indonesia, Chile, and Japan. These deposits are polycrystalline, with the largest documented single crystal measuring 22×16×11&#160;cm.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Historically, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> was a major source of sulfur in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Native sulfur is synthesised by <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_bacteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaerobic bacteria">anaerobic bacteria</a> acting on <a href="/wiki/Sulfate_minerals" title="Sulfate minerals">sulfate minerals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Salt_domes" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt domes">salt domes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Significant deposits in salt domes occur along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Evaporite" title="Evaporite">evaporites</a> in eastern Europe and western Asia. Native sulfur may be produced by geological processes alone. Fossil-based sulfur deposits from salt domes were until recently the basis for commercial production in the United States, Russia, Turkmenistan, and Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-Nehb_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nehb-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Currently, commercial production is still carried out in the Osiek mine in Poland. Such sources are now of secondary commercial importance, and most are no longer worked.
</p><p>Common naturally occurring sulfur compounds include the <a href="/wiki/Mineral#Sulfide_class" title="Mineral">sulfide minerals</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a> (iron sulfide), <a href="/wiki/Cinnabar" title="Cinnabar">cinnabar</a> (mercury sulfide), <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a> (lead sulfide), <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a> (zinc sulfide) and <a href="/wiki/Stibnite" title="Stibnite">stibnite</a> (antimony sulfide); and the sulfates, such as <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> (calcium sulfate), <a href="/wiki/Alunite" title="Alunite">alunite</a> (potassium aluminium sulfate), and <a href="/wiki/Barite" class="mw-redirect" title="Barite">barite</a> (barium sulfate). On Earth, just as upon Jupiter's moon Io, elemental sulfur occurs naturally in volcanic emissions, including emissions from <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Sulfur_compounds" title="Category:Sulfur compounds">Category:Sulfur compounds</a>.</div>
<p>Common <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> of sulfur range from −2 to +6. Sulfur forms stable compounds with all elements except the <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gases</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfur_polycations">Sulfur polycations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sulfur polycations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur <a href="/wiki/Polycation" class="mw-redirect" title="Polycation">polycations</a>, S<sub>8</sub><sup>2+</sup>, S<sub>4</sub><sup>2+</sup> and S<sub>16</sub><sup>2+</sup> are produced when sulfur is reacted with mild oxidising agents in a strongly acidic solution.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The colored solutions produced by dissolving sulfur in <a href="/wiki/Oleum" title="Oleum">oleum</a> were first reported as early as 1804 by C.F. Bucholz, but the cause of the color and the structure of the polycations involved was only determined in the late 1960s. S<sub>8</sub><sup>2+</sup> is deep blue, S<sub>4</sub><sup>2+</sup> is yellow and S<sub>16</sub><sup>2+</sup> is red.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfides">Sulfides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sulfides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Treatment of sulfur with hydrogen gives <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>. When dissolved in water, hydrogen sulfide is mildly acidic:<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwd_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwd-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>S ⇌ HS<sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Hydrogen sulfide gas and the hydrosulfide anion are extremely toxic to mammals, due to their inhibition of the oxygen-carrying capacity of hemoglobin and certain <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome" title="Cytochrome">cytochromes</a> in a manner analogous to <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azide" title="Azide">azide</a> (see below, under <i>precautions</i>).
</p><p>Reduction of elemental sulfur gives <a href="/wiki/Polysulfide" title="Polysulfide">polysulfides</a>, which consist of chains of sulfur atoms terminated with S<sup>−</sup> centers:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Na + S<sub>8</sub> → Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>8</sub></dd></dl>
<p>This reaction highlights a distinctive property of sulfur: its ability to <a href="/wiki/Catenation" title="Catenation">catenate</a> (bind to itself by formation of chains). <a href="/wiki/Protonation" title="Protonation">Protonation</a> of these polysulfide anions produces the <a href="/wiki/Polysulfane" title="Polysulfane">polysulfanes</a>, H<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> where x = 2, 3, and 4.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Ultimately, reduction of sulfur produces sulfide salts:
</p>
<dl><dd>16 Na + S<sub>8</sub> → 8 Na<sub>2</sub>S</dd></dl>
<p>The interconversion of these species is exploited in the <a href="/wiki/Sodium-sulfur_battery" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodium-sulfur battery">sodium-sulfur battery</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Trisulfur" title="Trisulfur">radical anion</a> S<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> gives the blue color of the mineral <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg/170px-Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg" width="170" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg/255px-Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg/340px-Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg 2x" data-file-width="673" data-file-height="1197" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lapis_lazuli_block.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">Lapis lazuli</a> owes its blue color to a <a href="/wiki/Trisulfur" title="Trisulfur">trisulfur</a> radical anion (<span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:S@CNT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/S%40CNT.jpg/220px-S%40CNT.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/S%40CNT.jpg/330px-S%40CNT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/S%40CNT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="487" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:S@CNT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two parallel sulfur chains grown inside a single-wall <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">carbon nanotube</a> (CNT, a). Zig-zag (b) and straight (c) S chains inside double-wall CNTs<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span id="Oxides.2C_oxoacids_and_oxoanions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Oxides,_oxoacids_and_oxoanions">Oxides, oxoacids and oxoanions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Oxides, oxoacids and oxoanions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The principal sulfur oxides are obtained by burning sulfur:
</p>
<dl><dd>S + O<sub>2</sub> → SO<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a>)</dd>
<dd>2 SO<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 SO<sub>3</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a>)</dd></dl>
<p>Multiple sulfur oxides are known; the <a href="/wiki/Lower_sulfur_oxides" title="Lower sulfur oxides">sulfur-rich oxides</a> include sulfur monoxide, disulfur monoxide, disulfur dioxides, and <a href="/wiki/Higher_sulfur_oxides" title="Higher sulfur oxides">higher oxides</a> containing peroxo groups.
</p><p>Sulfur forms <a href="/wiki/Oxoacids" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxoacids">sulfur oxoacids</a>, some of which cannot be isolated and are only known through the salts. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">Sulfur dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfites</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) are related to the unstable <a href="/wiki/Sulfurous_acid" title="Sulfurous acid">sulfurous acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>). <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">Sulfur trioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfates</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) are related to <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>). Sulfuric acid and SO<sub>3</sub> combine to give oleum, a solution of <a href="/wiki/Pyrosulfuric_acid" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrosulfuric acid">pyrosulfuric acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) in sulfuric acid.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Thiosulfate" title="Thiosulfate">Thiosulfate</a> salts (<span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), sometimes referred as "hyposulfites", used in <a href="/wiki/Photographic_fixer" title="Photographic fixer">photographic fixing</a> (hypo) and as reducing agents, feature sulfur in two oxidation states. <a href="/wiki/Sodium_dithionite" title="Sodium dithionite">Sodium dithionite</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">Na<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>), contains the more highly reducing <a href="/wiki/Dithionite" title="Dithionite">dithionite</a> anion (<span class="chemf nowrap">S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Halides_and_oxyhalides">Halides and oxyhalides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Halides and oxyhalides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several sulfur halides are important to modern industry. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_hexafluoride" title="Sulfur hexafluoride">Sulfur hexafluoride</a> is a dense gas used as an <a href="/wiki/Dielectric_gas" title="Dielectric gas">insulator gas</a> in high voltage <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformers</a>; it is also a nonreactive and nontoxic propellant for pressurized containers. <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_tetrafluoride" title="Sulfur tetrafluoride">Sulfur tetrafluoride</a> is a rarely used organic reagent that is highly toxic.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dichloride" title="Sulfur dichloride">Sulfur dichloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Disulfur_dichloride" title="Disulfur dichloride">disulfur dichloride</a> are important industrial chemicals. <a href="/wiki/Sulfuryl_chloride" title="Sulfuryl chloride">Sulfuryl chloride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorosulfuric_acid" title="Chlorosulfuric acid">chlorosulfuric acid</a> are derivatives of sulfuric acid; <a href="/wiki/Thionyl_chloride" title="Thionyl chloride">thionyl chloride</a> (SOCl<sub>2</sub>) is a common reagent in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pnictides">Pnictides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Pnictides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An important S–N compound is the cage <a href="/wiki/Tetrasulfur_tetranitride" title="Tetrasulfur tetranitride">tetrasulfur tetranitride</a> (S<sub>4</sub>N<sub>4</sub>). Heating this compound gives <a href="/wiki/Polythiazyl" title="Polythiazyl">polymeric sulfur nitride</a> ((SN)<sub>x</sub>), which has metallic properties even though it does not contain any <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metal</a> atoms. <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanate" title="Thiocyanate">Thiocyanates</a> contain the SCN<sup>−</sup> group. Oxidation of thiocyanate gives <a href="/wiki/Thiocyanogen" title="Thiocyanogen">thiocyanogen</a>, (SCN)<sub>2</sub> with the connectivity NCS-SCN. <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_sulfide" title="Phosphorus sulfide">Phosphorus sulfides</a> are numerous, the most important commercially being the cages P<sub>4</sub>S<sub>10</sub> and P<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chivers_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chivers-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metal_sulfides">Metal sulfides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Metal sulfides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sulfide_mineral" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulfide mineral">Sulfide mineral</a></div>
<p>The principal ores of copper, zinc, nickel, cobalt, molybdenum, and other metals are sulfides. These materials tend to be dark-colored <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a> that are not readily attacked by water or even many acids. They are formed, both <a href="/wiki/Geochemical_cycle" title="Geochemical cycle">geochemically</a> and in the laboratory, by the reaction of hydrogen sulfide with metal salts. The mineral <a href="/wiki/Galena" title="Galena">galena</a> (PbS) was the first demonstrated semiconductor and was used as a signal <a href="/wiki/Rectifier" title="Rectifier">rectifier</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Cat%27s-whisker_detector" class="mw-redirect" title="Cat&#39;s-whisker detector">cat's whiskers</a> of early <a href="/wiki/Crystal_radio" title="Crystal radio">crystal radios</a>. The iron sulfide called <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>, the so-called "fool's gold", has the formula FeS<sub>2</sub>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Processing these ores, usually by <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">roasting</a>, is costly and environmentally hazardous. Sulfur corrodes many metals through <a href="/wiki/Tarnishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Tarnishing">tarnishing</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds">Organic compounds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Organic compounds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organosulfur_compounds" title="Organosulfur compounds">Organosulfur compounds</a></div>

</p>
